<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Baltic Sea</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Baltic_(disambiguation)" title="Baltic (disambiguation)">Baltic (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A sea in Northern Europe bounded by the Scandinavian Peninsula, the mainland of Europe, and the Danish islands</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p>The <b>Baltic Sea</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginal_sea" title="Marginal sea">marginal sea</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, enclosed by <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, northwest <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/North_European_Plain" title="North European Plain">North and Central European Plain</a>.
</p><p>The sea stretches from <a href="/wiki/53rd_parallel_north" title="53rd parallel north">53°N</a> to <a href="/wiki/66th_parallel_north" title="66th parallel north">66°N</a> <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> and from <a href="/wiki/10th_meridian_east" title="10th meridian east">10°E</a> to <a href="/wiki/30th_meridian_east" title="30th meridian east">30°E</a> <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_sea_(oceanography)" title="Mediterranean sea (oceanography)">mediterranean sea</a> of the Atlantic, with limited water exchange between the two bodies, the Baltic Sea drains through the <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Danish</a> islands into the <a href="/wiki/Kattegat" title="Kattegat">Kattegat</a> by way of the straits of <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund" title="Øresund">Øresund</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Belt" title="Great Belt">Great Belt</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Little_Belt" title="Little Belt">Little Belt</a>.  It includes the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bothnia" title="Bay of Bothnia">Bay of Bothnia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Riga" title="Gulf of Riga">Gulf of Riga</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bay_of_Gda%C5%84sk" title="Bay of Gdańsk">Bay of Gdańsk</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Proper" title="Baltic Proper">Baltic Proper</a> is bordered on its northern edge, at the latitude <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_north" title="60th parallel north">60°N</a>, by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%85land_islands" title="Åland islands">Åland islands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a>, on its northeastern edge by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a>, on its eastern edge by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Riga" title="Gulf of Riga">Gulf of Riga</a>, and in the west by the <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> part of the southern Scandinavian Peninsula.
</p><p>The Baltic Sea is connected by artificial waterways to the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a> via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Sea_Canal" title="White Sea Canal">White Sea Canal</a> and to the <a href="/wiki/German_Bight" title="German Bight">German Bight</a> of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Kiel_Canal" title="Kiel Canal">Kiel Canal</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Belte_inter.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="566" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Belte_inter.png/290px-Belte_inter.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Belte_inter.png/435px-Belte_inter.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Belte_inter.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Belte_inter.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Danish Straits and southwestern Baltic Sea</div></div></div>
<p><b>Administration</b>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Convention_on_the_Protection_of_the_Marine_Environment_of_the_Baltic_Sea_Area" title="Helsinki Convention on the Protection of the Marine Environment of the Baltic Sea Area">Helsinki Convention on the Protection of the Marine Environment of the Baltic Sea Area</a> includes the Baltic Sea and the <a href="/wiki/Kattegat" title="Kattegat">Kattegat</a>, without calling Kattegat a part of the Baltic Sea, "For the purposes of this Convention the 'Baltic Sea Area' shall be the Baltic Sea and the Entrance to the Baltic Sea, bounded by the parallel of the Skaw in the Skagerrak at 57°44.43'N."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Traffic history</b>
</p><p>Historically, the Kingdom of Denmark collected <a href="/wiki/Sound_Dues" title="Sound Dues">Sound Dues</a> from ships at the border between the ocean and the land-locked Baltic Sea. They were collected in the <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund" title="Øresund">Øresund</a> at <a href="/wiki/Kronborg" title="Kronborg">Kronborg</a> castle near <a href="/wiki/Helsing%C3%B8r" title="Helsingør">Helsingør</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Belt" title="Great Belt">Great Belt</a> at <a href="/wiki/Nyborg" title="Nyborg">Nyborg</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Little_Belt" title="Little Belt">Little Belt</a>, the site of intake was moved to <a href="/wiki/Fredericia" title="Fredericia">Fredericia</a>, after that stronghold had been built. The narrowest part of Little Belt is the "Middelfart Sund" near <a href="/wiki/Middelfart" title="Middelfart">Middelfart</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Oceanography</b>
</p><p>Geographers widely agree that the preferred physical border of the Baltic is a line drawn through the southern Danish islands, <a href="/wiki/Drogden" title="Drogden">Drogden</a>-Sill and <a href="/wiki/Langeland" title="Langeland">Langeland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The Drogden Sill is situated north of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B8ge_Bugt" title="Køge Bugt">Køge Bugt</a> and connects <a href="/wiki/Drag%C3%B8r" title="Dragør">Dragør</a> in the south of <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> to <a href="/wiki/Malm%C3%B6" title="Malmö">Malmö</a>; it is used by the <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund_Bridge" title="Øresund Bridge">Øresund Bridge</a>, including the <i>Drogden Tunnel</i>. By this definition, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danish_Straits" title="Danish Straits">Danish Straits</a> are part of the entrance, but the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Mecklenburg" title="Bay of Mecklenburg">Bay of Mecklenburg</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Kiel" title="Bay of Kiel">Bay of Kiel</a> are parts of the Baltic Sea.
Another usual border is the line between <a href="/wiki/Falsterbo" title="Falsterbo">Falsterbo</a>, Sweden and <a href="/wiki/Stevns_Klint" title="Stevns Klint">Stevns Klint</a>, Denmark, as this is the southern border of <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund" title="Øresund">Øresund</a>. It's also the border between the shallow southern Øresund (with a typical depth of 5–10 meters only) and notably deeper water.
</p><p><b>Hydrography and biology</b>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Drogden" title="Drogden">Drogden</a> Sill (depth of 7 m (23 ft)) sets a limit to Øresund and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darss" title="Darss">Darss</a> Sill (depth of 18 m (59 ft)), and a limit to the Belt Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The shallow <a href="/wiki/Sill_(geology)" title="Sill (geology)">sills</a> are obstacles to the flow of heavy salt water from the Kattegat into the basins around <a href="/wiki/Bornholm" title="Bornholm">Bornholm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>.
</p><p>The Kattegat and the southwestern Baltic Sea are well oxygenated and have a rich biology. The remainder of the Sea is brackish, poor in oxygen and in species. Thus, statistically, the more of the entrance that is included in its definition, the healthier the Baltic appears; conversely, the more narrowly it is defined, the more endangered its biology appears.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> called it <i>Mare Suebicum</i> after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_people" title="Germanic people">Germanic people</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">Suebi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> <i>Sarmatian Ocean</i> after the <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> but the first to name it the <i>Baltic Sea</i> (<i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Mare_Balticum" title="wikt:Mare Balticum">Mare Balticum</a></i>) was the eleventh-century German chronicler <a href="/wiki/Adam_of_Bremen" title="Adam of Bremen">Adam of Bremen</a>. The origin of the latter name is speculative and it was adopted into <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic languages</a> spoken around the sea, very likely due to the role of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>. It might be connected to the Germanic word <i>belt</i>, a name used for two of the Danish straits, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Belts" title="The Belts">the Belts</a>, while others claim it to be directly derived from the source of the Germanic word, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>balteus</i> "belt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Latin-etymology_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Latin-etymology-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adam_of_Bremen" title="Adam of Bremen">Adam of Bremen</a> himself compared the sea with a belt, stating that it is so named because it stretches through the land as a belt (<i>Balticus, eo quod in modum baltei longo tractu per Scithicas regiones tendatur usque in Greciam</i>).
</p><p>He might also have been influenced by the name of a legendary island mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)"><i>Natural History</i></a> of <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>. Pliny mentions an island named <a href="/wiki/Baltia" title="Baltia">Baltia</a> (or Balcia) with reference to accounts of <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>. It is possible that Pliny refers to an island named Basilia ("the royal") in <i>On the Ocean</i> by Pytheas. <i>Baltia</i> also might be derived from <i>belt</i> and mean "near belt of sea, strait."
</p><p>Meanwhile, others have suggested that the name of the island originates from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> root <i>*bhel</i> meaning "white, fair".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> This root and its basic meaning were retained in both <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a> (as <i>baltas</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a> (as <i>balts</i>). On this basis, a related hypothesis holds that the name originated from this Indo-European root via a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltic_language" title="Baltic language">Baltic language</a> such as Lithuanian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Another explanation is that, while derived from the aforementioned root, the name of the sea is related to names for various forms of water and related substances in several European languages, that might have been originally associated with colors found in swamps (compare Proto-Slavic <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Reconstruction:Proto-Slavic/bolto" title="wikt:Reconstruction:Proto-Slavic/bolto">*bolto</a></i> "swamp"). Yet another explanation is that the name originally meant "enclosed sea, bay" as opposed to open sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baltu_valodas_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-baltu_valodas-12">[12]</a></sup> Some Swedish historians believe the name derives from the god <a href="/wiki/Baldr" title="Baldr">Baldr</a> of Nordic mythology.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> the sea was known by a variety of names. The name Baltic Sea became dominant only after 1600. Usage of <i>Baltic</i> and similar terms to denote the region east of the sea started only in 19th century.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_in_other_languages">Name in other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Name in other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Baltic Sea was known in ancient Latin language sources as <i>Mare Suebicum</i> or even <i>Mare Germanicum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Older native names in languages that used to be spoken on the shores of the sea or near it usually indicate the geographical location of the sea (in Germanic languages), or its size in relation to smaller gulfs (in Old Latvian), or tribes associated with it (in Old Russian the sea was known as the Varanghian Sea). In modern languages it is known by the equivalents of "East Sea", "West Sea", or "Baltic Sea" in different languages:
</p>
<ul><li><b>"Baltic Sea"</b> is used in Modern English; in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Baltic languages</a> <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a> (<i>Baltijas jūra</i>; in Old Latvian it was referred to as "the Big Sea", while the present day Gulf of Riga was referred to as "the Little Sea") and <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a> (<i>Baltijos jūra</i>); in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> (<i>Mare Balticum</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> (<i>Mer Baltique</i>), <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> (<i>Mar Baltico</i>), <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> (<i>Mar Báltico</i>), <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a> (<i>Marea Baltică</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> (<i>Mar Báltico</i>); in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> (Βαλτική Θάλασσα <i>Valtikí Thálassa</i>); in <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a> (<i>Deti Balltik</i>); in <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> (<i>Môr Baltig</i>); in the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a> <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> (<i>Morze Bałtyckie</i> or <i>Bałtyk</i>), <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a> (<i>Baltské moře</i> or <i>Balt</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slovenian_language" title="Slovenian language">Slovenian</a> (<i>Baltsko morje</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a> (Балтийско море <i>Baltijsko More</i>), <a href="/wiki/Kashubian_language" title="Kashubian language">Kashubian</a> (<i>Bôłt</i>), <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_language" title="Macedonian language">Macedonian</a> (Балтичко Море <i>Baltičko More</i>), <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a> (Балтійське море <i>Baltijs′ke More</i>), <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a> (Балтыйскае мора <i>Baltyjskaje Mora</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_(language)" title="Russian (language)">Russian</a> (Балтийское море <i>Baltiyskoye More</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian" title="Serbo-Croatian">Serbo-Croatian</a> (<i>Baltičko more</i> / Балтичко море); in <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> (Balti-tenger).</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>, except English, <b>"East Sea"</b> is used, as in <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a> (<i>Oossee</i>), <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> (<i>Østersøen</i>), <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> (<i>Oostzee</i>), <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> (<i>Ostsee</i>), <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a> (<i>Eystrasalt</i>), <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bokm%C3%A5l" title="Bokmål">Bokmål</a>: <i>Østersjøen</i>; <a href="/wiki/Nynorsk" title="Nynorsk">Nynorsk</a>: <i>Austersjøen</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a> (<i>Östersjön</i>). In <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> it was known as <i>Ostsǣ</i>; also in <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> the former name was <i>Keleti-tenger</i> ("East-sea", due to German influence). In addition, <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic language</a>, has <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calqued</a> the Swedish term as <i>Itämeri</i> "East Sea", disregarding the geography (the sea is west of Finland), though understandably since Finland was a part of Sweden from the Middle Ages until 1809.</li>
<li>In another Finnic language, <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a>, it is called the <b>"West Sea"</b> (<i>Läänemeri</i>), with the correct geography (the sea is west of Estonia). In <a href="/wiki/South_Estonian" title="South Estonian">South Estonian</a>, it has the meaning of both <b>"West Sea"</b> and <b>"Evening Sea"</b> (<i>Õdagumeri</i>).</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_world">Classical world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Classical world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the time of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, the Baltic Sea was known as the <i>Mare Suebicum</i> or <i>Mare Sarmaticum</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaius_Cornelius_Tacitus" title="Gaius Cornelius Tacitus">Tacitus</a> in his AD 98 <i>Agricola</i> and <i>Germania</i> described the Mare Suebicum, named for the <a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">Suebi</a> tribe, during the spring months, as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brackish" title="Brackish">brackish</a> sea where the ice broke apart and chunks floated about. The Suebi eventually migrated southwest to reside for a while in the Rhineland area of modern Germany, where their name survives in the historic region known as <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jordanes" title="Jordanes">Jordanes</a> called it the <i>Germanic Sea</i> in his work, the <i><a href="/wiki/Getica" title="Getica">Getica</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Putgarten,_Kap_Arkona_(2011-05-21)_3.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1919" data-file-width="2871" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Putgarten%2C_Kap_Arkona_%282011-05-21%29_3.JPG/220px-Putgarten%2C_Kap_Arkona_%282011-05-21%29_3.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Putgarten%2C_Kap_Arkona_%282011-05-21%29_3.JPG/330px-Putgarten%2C_Kap_Arkona_%282011-05-21%29_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Putgarten%2C_Kap_Arkona_%282011-05-21%29_3.JPG/440px-Putgarten%2C_Kap_Arkona_%282011-05-21%29_3.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Putgarten,_Kap_Arkona_(2011-05-21)_3.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cape_Arkona" title="Cape Arkona">Cape Arkona</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BCgen" title="Rügen">Rügen</a> in Germany, was a sacred site of the <a href="/wiki/Rani_(Slavic_tribe)" title="Rani (Slavic tribe)">Slavs</a> before Christianization.</div></div></div>
<p>In the early <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, Norse (Scandinavian) merchants built a trade empire all around the Baltic. Later, the Norse fought for control of the Baltic against <a href="/wiki/Wends" title="Wends">Wendish tribes</a> dwelling on the southern shore. The Norse also used the rivers of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> for trade routes, finding their way eventually to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and southern Russia. This Norse-dominated period is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a>.
</p><p>Since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking_age" title="Viking age">Viking age</a>, the Scandinavians have referred to the Baltic Sea as <i>Austmarr</i> ("Eastern Lake"). "Eastern Sea", appears in the <i><a href="/wiki/Heimskringla" title="Heimskringla">Heimskringla</a></i> and <i>Eystra salt</i> appears in <i><a href="/wiki/S%C3%B6rla_%C3%BE%C3%A1ttr" title="Sörla þáttr">Sörla þáttr</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Saxo_Grammaticus" title="Saxo Grammaticus">Saxo Grammaticus</a> recorded in <i><a href="/wiki/Gesta_Danorum" title="Gesta Danorum">Gesta Danorum</a></i> an older name, <i><a href="/wiki/Gandvik" title="Gandvik">Gandvik</a></i>, <i>-vik</i> being <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> for "bay", which implies that the Vikings correctly regarded it as an inlet of the sea. Another form of the name, "Grandvik", attested in at least one English translation of <i>Gesta Danorum</i>, is likely to be a misspelling.)
</p><p>In addition to fish the sea also provides <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>, especially from its southern shores within today's borders of <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>. First mentions of amber deposits on the South coast of the Baltic Sea date back to the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The bordering countries have also traditionally exported lumber, <a href="/wiki/Pine_tar" title="Pine tar">wood tar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a> and furs by ship across the Baltic. Sweden had from early medieval times exported <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> mined there, while Poland had and still has extensive <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> mines. Thus the Baltic Sea has long been crossed by much merchant shipping.
</p><p>The lands on the Baltic's eastern shore were among the last in Europe to be converted to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. This finally happened during the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">Northern Crusades</a>: <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> in the twelfth century by Swedes, and what are now <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> in the early thirteenth century by Danes and Germans (<a href="/wiki/Livonian_Brothers_of_the_Sword" title="Livonian Brothers of the Sword">Livonian Brothers of the Sword</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutonic Order</a> gained control over parts of the southern and eastern shore of the Baltic Sea, where they set up <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monastic_state_of_the_Teutonic_Knights" title="Monastic state of the Teutonic Knights">their monastic state</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> was <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Lithuania" title="Christianization of Lithuania">the last European state to convert to Christianity</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="An_arena_of_conflict">An arena of conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: An arena of conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haupthandelsroute_Hanse.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="603" data-file-width="789" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Haupthandelsroute_Hanse.png/220px-Haupthandelsroute_Hanse.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Haupthandelsroute_Hanse.png/330px-Haupthandelsroute_Hanse.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Haupthandelsroute_Hanse.png/440px-Haupthandelsroute_Hanse.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haupthandelsroute_Hanse.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main trading routes of the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> (<i>Hanse</i>).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Curonians_kursenieki_in_1649.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Curonians_kursenieki_in_1649.png/220px-Curonians_kursenieki_in_1649.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Curonians_kursenieki_in_1649.png/330px-Curonians_kursenieki_in_1649.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Curonians_kursenieki_in_1649.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Curonians_kursenieki_in_1649.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1649 the settlement of the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian-speaking</a> <a href="/wiki/Kursenieki" title="Kursenieki">Kursenieki</a> spanned from <a href="/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da" title="Klaipėda">Klaipėda</a> to <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a> along the coast of the Baltic Sea.</div></div></div>
<p>In the period between the 8th and 14th centuries, there was much piracy in the Baltic  from the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Pomerania" title="Pomerania">Pomerania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Victual_Brothers" title="Victual Brothers">Victual Brothers</a> even held <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>.
</p><p>Starting in the 11th century, the southern and eastern shores of the Baltic were settled by migrants mainly from <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, a movement called the <i><a href="/wiki/Ostsiedlung" title="Ostsiedlung">Ostsiedlung</a></i> ("east settling"). Other settlers were from the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Polabian_Slavs" title="Polabian Slavs">Polabian Slavs</a> were gradually assimilated by the Germans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> gradually gained control over most of the Baltic coast, until she lost much of her possessions after being defeated in the 1227 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bornh%C3%B6ved_(1227)" title="Battle of Bornhöved (1227)">Battle of Bornhöved</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_(Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten,_1660).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="647" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg/220px-Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg/330px-Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg/440px-Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_%28Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten%2C_1660%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eerste_fase_van_de_Zeeslag_in_de_Sont_-_First_phase_of_the_Battle_of_the_Sound_-_November_8_1658_(Jan_Abrahamsz_Beerstraten,_1660).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The naval <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Sound" title="Battle of the Sound">Battle of the Sound</a> took place on 8 November 1658 during the <a href="/wiki/Dano-Swedish_War_(1658%E2%80%9360)" title="Dano-Swedish War (1658–60)">Dano-Swedish War</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cap_Arcona_burning.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="172" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Cap_Arcona_burning.jpg/220px-Cap_Arcona_burning.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/03/Cap_Arcona_burning.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cap_Arcona_burning.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The burning <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cap_Arcona" title="Cap Arcona">Cap Arcona</a></i> shortly after the attacks, 3 May 1945. Only 350 survived of the 4,500 prisoners who had been aboard</div></div></div>
<p>In the 13th to 16th centuries, the strongest economic force in Northern Europe was the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, a federation of merchant cities around the Baltic Sea and the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. In the sixteenth and early seventeenth centuries, <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> fought wars for <i><a href="/wiki/Dominium_maris_baltici" title="Dominium maris baltici">Dominium maris baltici</a></i> ("Lordship over the Baltic Sea"). Eventually, it was Sweden that <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Empire" title="Swedish Empire">virtually encompassed the Baltic Sea</a>. In Sweden the sea was then referred to as <i>Mare Nostrum Balticum</i> ("Our Baltic Sea"). The goal of Swedish warfare during the 17th century was to make the Baltic Sea an all-Swedish sea (<i>Ett Svenskt innanhav</i>), something that was accomplished except the part between Riga in Latvia and <a href="/wiki/Szczecin" title="Szczecin">Stettin</a> in Pomerania.  However, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch</a> dominated Baltic trade in the seventeenth century.
</p><p>In the eighteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> became the leading powers over the sea. Sweden's defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_War" title="Great Northern War">Great Northern War</a> brought Russia to the eastern coast. Russia became and remained a dominating power in the Baltic. Russia's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_I_of_Russia" title="Peter I of Russia">Peter the Great</a> saw the strategic importance of the Baltic and decided to found his new capital, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, at the mouth of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neva" title="Neva">Neva</a> river at the east end of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a>. There was much trading not just within the Baltic region but also with the North Sea region, especially eastern <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>: their fleets needed the Baltic timber, tar, flax and hemp.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, a joint <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> fleet attacked the Russian fortresses in the Baltic. They bombarded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sveaborg" title="Sveaborg">Sveaborg</a>, which guards <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kronstadt,_Russia" title="Kronstadt, Russia">Kronstadt</a>, which guards Saint Petersburg; and they destroyed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bomarsund,_%C3%85land" title="Bomarsund, Åland">Bomarsund</a> in the <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">Åland Islands</a>. After the unification of <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> in 1871, the whole southern coast became German. <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> was partly fought in the Baltic Sea. After 1920 <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> was connected to the Baltic Sea by the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Corridor" title="Polish Corridor">Polish Corridor</a> and enlarged the port of <a href="/wiki/Gdynia" title="Gdynia">Gdynia</a> in rivalry with the port of the <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Danzig" title="Free City of Danzig">Free City of Danzig</a>.
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Germany reclaimed all of the southern and much of the eastern shore by occupying Poland and the Baltic states. In 1945, the Baltic Sea became a mass grave for retreating soldiers and refugees on torpedoed <a href="/wiki/Operation_Hannibal" title="Operation Hannibal">troop transports</a>. The sinking of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Gustloff_(ship)" title="Wilhelm Gustloff (ship)">Wilhelm Gustloff</a></i> remains the worst maritime disaster in history, killing (very roughly) 9,000 people. In 2005, a Russian group of scientists found over five thousand airplane wrecks, sunken warships, and other material, mainly from World War II, on the bottom of the sea.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_World_War_II">Since World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Since World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, various nations, including the Soviet Union, the United Kingdom and the United States, have disposed of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_warfare" title="Chemical warfare">chemical weapons</a> in the Baltic Sea, raising concerns of environmental contamination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Even now fishermen accidentally retrieve some of these materials: the most recent available report from the Helsinki Commission notes that four small scale catches of chemical munitions representing approximately 105 kg (231 lb) of material were reported in 2005. This is a reduction from the 25 incidents representing 1,110 kg (2,450 lb) of material in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Until now, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Government" title="U.S. Government">U.S. Government</a> refuses to disclose the exact coordinates of the wreck sites. Rotting bottles leak <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mustard_gas" title="Mustard gas">mustard gas</a> and other substances, thus slowly poisoning a substantial part of the Baltic Sea.
</p><p>After 1945, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_and_expulsion_of_Germans_(1944%E2%80%931950)" title="Flight and expulsion of Germans (1944–1950)">German population was expelled</a> from all areas east of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oder-Neisse_line" title="Oder-Neisse line">Oder-Neisse line</a>, making room for displaced Poles and Russians. Poland <a href="/wiki/Former_eastern_territories_of_Germany" title="Former eastern territories of Germany">gained most of the southern shore</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> gained another access to the Baltic with the <a href="/wiki/Kaliningrad_Oblast" title="Kaliningrad Oblast">Kaliningrad Oblast</a>. The Baltic states on the eastern shore were annexed by the Soviet Union. The Baltic then separated opposing military blocs: <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a>. Had war broken out, the Polish navy was prepared to invade the Danish isles. This border status restricted trade and travel. It ended only after the collapse of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communist" title="Communist">Communist</a> regimes in Central and Eastern Europe in the late 1980s.
</p><p>Since May 2004, with the accession of the Baltic states and Poland, the Baltic Sea has been almost entirely surrounded by countries of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU). The only remaining non-EU shore areas are Russian: the Saint Petersburg area and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exclave" title="Exclave">exclave</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kaliningrad_Oblast" title="Kaliningrad Oblast">Kaliningrad Oblast</a>.
</p><p>Winter storms begin arriving in the region during October. These have caused numerous shipwrecks, and contributed to the extreme difficulties of rescuing passengers of the ferry <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M/S_Estonia" title="M/S Estonia">M/S Estonia</a></i> en route from <a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">Tallinn</a>, Estonia, to <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, Sweden, in September 1994, which claimed the lives of 852 people. Older, wood-based shipwrecks such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Vasa_(ship)" title="Vasa (ship)">Vasa</a></i> tend to remain well-preserved, as the Baltic's cold and brackish water does not suit the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shipworm" title="Shipworm">shipworm</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storm_floods">Storm floods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Storm floods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">Storm surge</a> floodings are generally taken to occur when the water level is more than one metre above normal. In Warnemünde about 110 floods occurred from 1950 to 2000, an average of just over two per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historic flood events were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Flood_of_1304" title="All Saints' Flood of 1304">All Saints' Flood of 1304</a> and other floods in the years 1320, 1449, 1625, 1694, 1784 and 1825. Little is known of their extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> From 1872, there exist regular and reliable records of water levels in the Baltic Sea. The highest was the <a href="/wiki/1872_Baltic_Sea_flood" title="1872 Baltic Sea flood">flood of 1872</a> when the water was an average of 2.43 m (8 ft 0 in) above sea level at Warnemünde and a maximum of 2.83 m (9 ft 3 in) above sea level in Warnemünde. In the last very heavy floods the average water levels reached 1.88 m (6 ft 2 in) above sea level in 1904, 1.89 m (6 ft 2 in) in 1913, 1.73 m (5 ft 8 in) in January 1954, 1.68 m (5 ft 6 in) on 2–4 November 1995 and 1.65 m (5 ft 5 in) on 21 February 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geophysical_data">Geophysical data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geophysical data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baltic_drainage_basins_(catchment_area).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="666" data-file-width="516" decoding="async" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Baltic_drainage_basins_%28catchment_area%29.svg/220px-Baltic_drainage_basins_%28catchment_area%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Baltic_drainage_basins_%28catchment_area%29.svg/330px-Baltic_drainage_basins_%28catchment_area%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Baltic_drainage_basins_%28catchment_area%29.svg/440px-Baltic_drainage_basins_%28catchment_area%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baltic_drainage_basins_(catchment_area).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Baltic drainage basins (catchment area), with depth, elevation, major rivers and lakes</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Curonian_Spit_NP_05-2017_img17_aerial_view_at_Epha_Dune.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2633" data-file-width="4213" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Curonian_Spit_NP_05-2017_img17_aerial_view_at_Epha_Dune.jpg/220px-Curonian_Spit_NP_05-2017_img17_aerial_view_at_Epha_Dune.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Curonian_Spit_NP_05-2017_img17_aerial_view_at_Epha_Dune.jpg/330px-Curonian_Spit_NP_05-2017_img17_aerial_view_at_Epha_Dune.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Curonian_Spit_NP_05-2017_img17_aerial_view_at_Epha_Dune.jpg/440px-Curonian_Spit_NP_05-2017_img17_aerial_view_at_Epha_Dune.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Curonian_Spit_NP_05-2017_img17_aerial_view_at_Epha_Dune.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Curonian_Spit" title="Curonian Spit">Curonian Spit</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kaliningrad_Oblast" title="Kaliningrad Oblast">Kaliningrad Oblast</a>, Russia</div></div></div>
<p>An arm of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Ocean" title="North Atlantic Ocean">North Atlantic Ocean</a>, the Baltic Sea is enclosed by <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> to the northeast, the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic countries</a> to the southeast, and the <a href="/wiki/North_European_Plain" title="North European Plain">North European Plain</a> to the southwest.
</p><p>It is about 1,600 km (990 mi) long, an average of 193 km (120 mi) wide, and an average of 55 metres (180 ft) deep. The maximum depth is 459 m (1,506 ft) which is on the Swedish side of the center. The surface area is about 349,644 km<sup>2</sup> (134,998 sq mi) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and the volume is about 20,000 km<sup>3</sup> (4,800 cu mi). The periphery amounts to about 8,000 km (5,000 mi) of coastline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Baltic Sea is one of the largest <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">brackish</a> inland seas by area, and occupies a basin (a <i><a href="/wiki/Zungenbecken" title="Zungenbecken">zungenbecken</a></i>) formed by glacial erosion during the last few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_Age" title="Ice Age">ice ages</a>.
</p><p><b>Physical characteristics of the Baltic Sea, its main sub-regions, and the transition zone to the Skagerrak/North Sea area</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
