<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent">Extent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Extent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a> defines the limits of the Baltic Sea as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Bordered by the coasts of Germany, Denmark, Poland, Sweden, Finland, Russia, Estonia, Latvia and Lithuania, it extends north-eastward of the following limits:
<ul><li><i>In the <a href="/wiki/Little_Belt" title="Little Belt">Little Belt</a>.</i> A line joining <a href="/wiki/Nieby" title="Nieby">Falshöft</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Baltic_Sea&amp;params=54_47_N_9_57.5_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">54°47′N</span> <span class="longitude">9°57.5′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">54.783°N 9.9583°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">54.783; 9.9583</span></span></span></a></span>) and Vejsnæs Nakke (<a href="/wiki/%C3%86r%C3%B8" title="Ærø">Ærø</a>: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Baltic_Sea&amp;params=54_49_N_10_26_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">54°49′N</span> <span class="longitude">10°26′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">54.817°N 10.433°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">54.817; 10.433</span></span></span></a></span>).</li>
<li><i>In the <a href="/wiki/Great_Belt" title="Great Belt">Great Belt</a>.</i> A line joining Gulstav (South extreme of <a href="/wiki/Langeland" title="Langeland">Langeland</a> Island) and Kappel Kirke (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Baltic_Sea&amp;params=54_46_N_11_01_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">54°46′N</span> <span class="longitude">11°01′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">54.767°N 11.017°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">54.767; 11.017</span></span></span></a></span>) on Island of <a href="/wiki/Lolland" title="Lolland">Lolland</a>.</li>
<li><i>In the <a href="/wiki/Guldborgsund" title="Guldborgsund">Guldborg Sound</a>.</i> A line joining Flinthorne-Rev and Skjelby (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Baltic_Sea&amp;params=54_38_N_11_53_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">54°38′N</span> <span class="longitude">11°53′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">54.633°N 11.883°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">54.633; 11.883</span></span></span></a></span>).</li>
<li><i>In <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund" title="Øresund">the Sound</a>.</i> A line joining <a href="/wiki/Stevns_Peninsula" title="Stevns Peninsula">Stevns</a> Lighthouse (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Baltic_Sea&amp;params=55_17_N_12_27_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">55°17′N</span> <span class="longitude">12°27′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">55.283°N 12.450°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">55.283; 12.450</span></span></span></a></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Falsterbo" title="Falsterbo">Falsterbo Point</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Baltic_Sea&amp;params=55_23_N_12_49_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">55°23′N</span> <span class="longitude">12°49′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">55.383°N 12.817°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">55.383; 12.817</span></span></span></a></span>).</li></ul></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subdivisions">Subdivisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Subdivisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baltic_marine_subdivisions_and_drainage_basins.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="547" data-file-width="561" decoding="async" height="341" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Baltic_marine_subdivisions_and_drainage_basins.gif/350px-Baltic_marine_subdivisions_and_drainage_basins.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Baltic_marine_subdivisions_and_drainage_basins.gif/525px-Baltic_marine_subdivisions_and_drainage_basins.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b3/Baltic_marine_subdivisions_and_drainage_basins.gif 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baltic_marine_subdivisions_and_drainage_basins.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Regions and basins of the Baltic Sea:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><br/>1 = <a href="/wiki/Bothnian_Bay" title="Bothnian Bay">Bothnian Bay</a><br/>2 = <a href="/wiki/Bothnian_Sea" title="Bothnian Sea">Bothnian Sea</a><br/>1 + 2 = <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a>, partly also 3 &amp; 4<br/>3 = <a href="/wiki/Archipelago_Sea" title="Archipelago Sea">Archipelago Sea</a><br/>4 = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Sea" title="Åland Sea">Åland Sea</a><br/>5 = <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a><br/>6 = Northern Baltic Proper<br/>7 = Western <a href="/wiki/Gotland_Basin" title="Gotland Basin">Gotland Basin</a><br/>8 = Eastern <a href="/wiki/Gotland_Basin" title="Gotland Basin">Gotland Basin</a><br/>9 = <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Riga" title="Gulf of Riga">Gulf of Riga</a><br/>10 = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bay_of_Gda%C5%84sk" title="Bay of Gdańsk">Bay of Gdańsk</a>/Gdansk Basin<br/>11 = <a href="/wiki/Bornholm" title="Bornholm">Bornholm</a> Basin and <a href="/wiki/Han%C3%B6bukten" title="Hanöbukten">Hanö Bight</a><br/>12 = <a href="/wiki/Cape_Arkona" title="Cape Arkona">Arkona</a> Basin<br/>6–12 = <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Proper" title="Baltic Proper">Baltic Proper</a><br/>13 = <a href="/wiki/Kattegat" title="Kattegat">Kattegat</a>, not an integral part of the Baltic Sea<br/>14 = Belt Sea (<a href="/wiki/Little_Belt" title="Little Belt">Little Belt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_Belt" title="Great Belt">Great Belt</a>)<br/>15 = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96resund" title="Öresund">Öresund</a> (The Sound)<br/>14 + 15 = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danish_Straits" title="Danish Straits">Danish Straits</a>, not an integral part of the Baltic Sea</div></div></div>
<p>The northern part of the Baltic Sea is known as the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a>, of which the northernmost part is the Bay of Bothnia  or <a href="/wiki/Bothnian_Bay" title="Bothnian Bay">Bothnian Bay</a>. The more rounded southern basin of the gulf is called <a href="/wiki/Bothnian_Sea" title="Bothnian Sea">Bothnian Sea</a> and immediately to the south of it lies the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_%C3%85land" title="Sea of Åland">Sea of Åland</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a> connects the Baltic Sea with <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Riga" title="Gulf of Riga">Gulf of Riga</a> lies between the <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvian</a> capital city of <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonian</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Saaremaa" title="Saaremaa">Saaremaa</a>.
</p><p>The Northern Baltic Sea lies between the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> area, southwestern Finland and Estonia. The <a href="/wiki/Gotland_Basin" title="Gotland Basin">Western and Eastern Gotland Basins</a> form the major parts of the Central Baltic Sea or Baltic proper. The <a href="/wiki/Bornholm" title="Bornholm">Bornholm</a> Basin is the area east of Bornholm, and the shallower <a href="/wiki/Cape_Arkona" title="Cape Arkona">Arkona</a> Basin extends from Bornholm to the Danish isles of <a href="/wiki/Falster" title="Falster">Falster</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zealand_(Denmark)" title="Zealand (Denmark)">Zealand</a>.
</p><p>In the south, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bay_of_Gda%C5%84sk" title="Bay of Gdańsk">Bay of Gdańsk</a> lies east of the <a href="/wiki/Hel_Peninsula" title="Hel Peninsula">Hel Peninsula</a> on the Polish coast and west of the <a href="/wiki/Sambia_Peninsula" title="Sambia Peninsula">Sambia Peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kaliningrad_Oblast" title="Kaliningrad Oblast">Kaliningrad Oblast</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pomerania" title="Bay of Pomerania">Bay of Pomerania</a> lies north of the islands of <a href="/wiki/Usedom" title="Usedom">Usedom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolin" title="Wolin">Wolin</a>, east of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BCgen" title="Rügen">Rügen</a>. Between Falster and the German coast lie the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Mecklenburg" title="Bay of Mecklenburg">Bay of Mecklenburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_L%C3%BCbeck" title="Bay of Lübeck">Bay of Lübeck</a>. The westernmost part of the Baltic Sea is the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Kiel" title="Bay of Kiel">Bay of Kiel</a>. The three <a href="/wiki/Danish_straits" title="Danish straits">Danish straits</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Belt" title="Great Belt">Great Belt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Little_Belt" title="Little Belt">Little Belt</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96resund" title="Öresund">The Sound</a> (<i>Öresund</i>/<i>Øresund</i>), connect the Baltic Sea with the <a href="/wiki/Kattegat" title="Kattegat">Kattegat</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Skagerrak</a> strait in the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature_and_ice">Temperature and ice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Temperature and ice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BalticSea_March2000_NASA-S2000084115409.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1548" data-file-width="1118" decoding="async" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BalticSea_March2000_NASA-S2000084115409.png/220px-BalticSea_March2000_NASA-S2000084115409.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BalticSea_March2000_NASA-S2000084115409.png/330px-BalticSea_March2000_NASA-S2000084115409.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BalticSea_March2000_NASA-S2000084115409.png/440px-BalticSea_March2000_NASA-S2000084115409.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BalticSea_March2000_NASA-S2000084115409.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite image of the Baltic Sea in a mild winter</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baltic_Sea_1551_(5496745361).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3689" data-file-width="5579" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Baltic_Sea_1551_%285496745361%29.jpg/220px-Baltic_Sea_1551_%285496745361%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Baltic_Sea_1551_%285496745361%29.jpg/330px-Baltic_Sea_1551_%285496745361%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Baltic_Sea_1551_%285496745361%29.jpg/440px-Baltic_Sea_1551_%285496745361%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baltic_Sea_1551_(5496745361).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traversing Baltic Sea and ice</div></div></div>
<p>The water temperature of the Baltic Sea varies significantly depending on exact location, season and depth. At the Bornholm Basin, which is located directly east of the island of the same name, the surface temperature typically falls to 0–5 °C (32–41 °F) during the peak of the winter and rises to 15–20 °C (59–68 °F) during the peak of the summer, with an annual average of around 9–10 °C (48–50 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OurBalticSea_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-OurBalticSea-26">[26]</a></sup> A similar pattern can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/Gotland_Basin" title="Gotland Basin">Gotland Basin</a>, which is located between the island of Gotland and Latvia. In the deep of these basins the temperature variations are smaller. At the bottom of the Bornholm Basin, deeper than 80 m (260 ft), the temperature typically is 1–10 °C (34–50 °F), and at the bottom of the Gotland Basin, at depths greater than 225 m (738 ft), the temperature typically is 4–7 °C (39–45 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OurBalticSea_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-OurBalticSea-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the long-term average, the Baltic Sea is ice-covered at the annual maximum for about 45% of its surface area. The ice-covered area during such a typical winter includes the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Riga" title="Gulf of Riga">Gulf of Riga</a>, the archipelago west of Estonia, the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_archipelago" title="Stockholm archipelago">Stockholm archipelago</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Archipelago_Sea" title="Archipelago Sea">Archipelago Sea</a> southwest of Finland. The remainder of the Baltic does not freeze during a normal winter, except sheltered bays and shallow lagoons such as the <a href="/wiki/Curonian_Lagoon" title="Curonian Lagoon">Curonian Lagoon</a>. The ice reaches its maximum extent in February or March; typical ice thickness in the northernmost areas in the <a href="/wiki/Bothnian_Bay" title="Bothnian Bay">Bothnian Bay</a>, the northern basin of the Gulf of Bothnia, is about 70 cm (28 in) for landfast sea ice. The thickness decreases farther south.
</p><p>Freezing begins in the northern extremities of the Gulf of Bothnia typically in the middle of November, reaching the open waters of the Bothnian Bay in early January. The <a href="/wiki/Bothnian_Sea" title="Bothnian Sea">Bothnian Sea</a>, the basin south of <a href="/wiki/Kvarken" title="Kvarken">Kvarken</a>, freezes on average in late February. The Gulf of Finland and the Gulf of Riga freeze typically in late January. In 2011, the Gulf of Finland was completely frozen on 15 February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ice extent depends on whether the winter is mild, moderate, or severe. In severe winters ice can form around southern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and even in the <a href="/wiki/Danish_straits" title="Danish straits">Danish straits</a>. According to the 18th-century natural historian <a href="/wiki/William_Derham" title="William Derham">William Derham</a>, during the severe winters of 1703 and 1708, the ice cover reached as far as the Danish straits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Frequently, parts of the Gulf of Bothnia and Gulf of Finland are frozen, in addition to coastal fringes in more southerly locations such as the Gulf of Riga. This description meant that the whole of the Baltic Sea was covered with ice.
</p><p>Since 1720, the Baltic Sea has frozen over entirely 20 times, most recently in early 1987, which was the most severe winter in Scandinavia since 1720. The ice then covered 400,000 km<sup>2</sup> (150,000 sq mi). During the winter of 2010–11, which was quite severe compared to those of the last decades, the maximum ice cover was 315,000 km<sup>2</sup> (122,000 sq mi), which was reached on 25 February 2011. The ice then extended from the north down to the northern tip of <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>, with small ice-free areas on either side, and the east coast of the Baltic Sea was covered by an ice sheet about 25 to 100 km (16 to 62 mi) wide all the way to <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a>. This was brought about by a stagnant <a href="/wiki/High-pressure_area" title="High-pressure area">high-pressure area</a> that lingered over central and northern Scandinavia from around 10 to 24 February. After this, strong southern winds pushed the ice further into the north, and much of the waters north of Gotland were again free of ice, which had then packed against the shores of southern Finland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The effects of the afore-mentioned high-pressure area did not reach the southern parts of the Baltic Sea, and thus the entire sea did not freeze over. However, floating ice was additionally observed near <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Awinouj%C5%9Bcie" title="Świnoujście">Świnoujście</a> harbour in January 2010.
</p><p>In recent years before 2011, the Bothnian Bay and the Bothnian Sea were frozen with solid ice near the Baltic coast and dense floating ice far from it. In 2008, almost no ice formed except for a short period in March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:J%C3%A4%C3%A4m%C3%A4ed_Puhtulaiul_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1184" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/J%C3%A4%C3%A4m%C3%A4ed_Puhtulaiul_1.JPG/220px-J%C3%A4%C3%A4m%C3%A4ed_Puhtulaiul_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/J%C3%A4%C3%A4m%C3%A4ed_Puhtulaiul_1.JPG/330px-J%C3%A4%C3%A4m%C3%A4ed_Puhtulaiul_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/J%C3%A4%C3%A4m%C3%A4ed_Puhtulaiul_1.JPG/440px-J%C3%A4%C3%A4m%C3%A4ed_Puhtulaiul_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:J%C3%A4%C3%A4m%C3%A4ed_Puhtulaiul_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Piles of drift ice on the shore of Puhtulaid, near <a href="/wiki/Virtsu" title="Virtsu">Virtsu</a>, Estonia, in late April</div></div></div>
<p>During winter, <a href="/wiki/Fast_ice" title="Fast ice">fast ice</a>, which is attached to the shoreline, develops first, rendering ports unusable without the services of <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreakers</a>. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Level_ice&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Level ice (page does not exist)">Level ice</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ice_sludge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ice sludge (page does not exist)">ice sludge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancake_ice" title="Pancake ice">pancake ice</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rafter_ice&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rafter ice (page does not exist)">rafter ice</a> form in the more open regions. The gleaming expanse of ice is similar to the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, with wind-driven pack ice and ridges up to 15 m (49 ft). Offshore of the landfast ice, the ice remains very dynamic all year, and it is relatively easily moved around by winds and therefore forms <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pack_ice" title="Pack ice">pack ice</a>, made up of large piles and ridges pushed against the landfast ice and shores.
</p><p>In spring, the Gulf of Finland and the Gulf of Bothnia normally thaw in late April, with some ice ridges persisting until May in the eastern extremities of the Gulf of Finland. In the northernmost reaches of the Bothnian Bay, ice usually stays until late May; by early June it is practically always gone. However, in the famine year of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Famine_of_1866-68" title="Famine of 1866-68">1867</a> remnants of ice were observed as late as July 17 near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uddsk%C3%A4r" title="Uddskär">Uddskär</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Even as far south as <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund" title="Øresund">Øresund</a>, remnants of ice have been observed in May on several occasions; near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taarbaek" title="Taarbaek">Taarbaek</a> on May 15, 1942 and near Copenhagen on May 11, 1771. Drift ice was also observed on May 11, 1799.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ice cover is the main habitat for two large mammals, the <a href="/wiki/Grey_seal" title="Grey seal">grey seal</a> (<i>Halichoerus grypus</i>) and the Baltic <a href="/wiki/Ringed_seal" title="Ringed seal">ringed seal</a> (<i>Pusa hispida botnica</i>), both of which feed underneath the ice and breed on its surface. Of these two seals, only the Baltic ringed seal suffers when there is not adequate ice in the Baltic Sea, as it feeds its young only while on ice. The grey seal is adapted to reproducing also with no ice in the sea. The sea ice also harbours several species of algae that live in the bottom and inside unfrozen brine pockets in the ice.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrography">Hydrography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Hydrography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ostseetiefen.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="985" data-file-width="826" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Ostseetiefen.png/220px-Ostseetiefen.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Ostseetiefen.png/330px-Ostseetiefen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Ostseetiefen.png/440px-Ostseetiefen.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ostseetiefen.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depths of the Baltic Sea in metres</div></div></div>
<p>The Baltic Sea flows out through the <a href="/wiki/Danish_straits" title="Danish straits">Danish straits</a>; however, the flow is complex. A surface layer of brackish water discharges 940 km<sup>3</sup> (230 cu mi) per year into the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. Due to the difference in <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a>, by salinity permeation principle, a sub-surface layer of more saline water moving in the opposite direction brings in 475 km<sup>3</sup> (114 cu mi) per year. It mixes very slowly with the upper waters, resulting in a salinity gradient from top to bottom, with most of the salt water remaining below 40 to 70 m (130 to 230 ft) deep. The general circulation is anti-clockwise: northwards along its eastern boundary, and south along the western one .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alhonen_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alhonen-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The difference between the outflow and the inflow comes entirely from fresh <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>. More than 250 streams drain a basin of about 1,600,000 km<sup>2</sup> (620,000 sq mi), contributing a volume of 660 km<sup>3</sup> (160 cu mi) per year to the Baltic. They include the major rivers of north Europe, such as the <a href="/wiki/Oder" title="Oder">Oder</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vistula" title="Vistula">Vistula</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neman_River" title="Neman River">Neman</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daugava_River" title="Daugava River">Daugava</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neva" title="Neva">Neva</a>. Additional fresh water comes from the difference of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> less evaporation, which is positive.
</p><p>An important source of salty water are infrequent inflows of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> water into the Baltic. Such inflows, important to the Baltic ecosystem because of the oxygen they transport into the Baltic deeps, used to happen regularly until the 1980s. In recent decades they have become less frequent. The latest four occurred in 1983, 1993, 2003 and 2014 suggesting a new inter-inflow period of about ten years.
</p><p>The water level is generally far more dependent on the regional wind situation than on tidal effects. However, tidal currents occur in narrow passages in the western parts of the Baltic Sea.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Significant_wave_height" title="Significant wave height">significant wave height</a> is generally much lower than that of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. Violent and sudden storms often sweep the surface, due to large transient temperature differences and a long reach of wind. Seasonal winds also cause small changes in sea level, of the order of 0.5 m (1 ft 8 in) .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alhonen_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alhonen-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salinity">Salinity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Salinity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunny_day_on_the_coast_on_Baltic_sea.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2750" data-file-width="4739" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Sunny_day_on_the_coast_on_Baltic_sea.jpg/220px-Sunny_day_on_the_coast_on_Baltic_sea.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Sunny_day_on_the_coast_on_Baltic_sea.jpg/330px-Sunny_day_on_the_coast_on_Baltic_sea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Sunny_day_on_the_coast_on_Baltic_sea.jpg/440px-Sunny_day_on_the_coast_on_Baltic_sea.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunny_day_on_the_coast_on_Baltic_sea.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Baltic Sea near <a href="/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da" title="Klaipėda">Klaipėda</a> (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Karkl%C4%97&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Karklė (page does not exist)">Karklė</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>The Baltic Sea is the world's largest inland <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brackish" title="Brackish">brackish</a> sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leijonmalm2017_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leijonmalm2017-36">[36]</a></sup>  Only two <a href="/wiki/List_of_brackish_bodies_of_water" title="List of brackish bodies of water">other brackish waters</a> are larger on some measurements: The <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> is larger in both surface area and water volume, but most of it is located outside the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a> (only a small percentage is inland). The <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> is larger in water volume, but—despite its name—it is a lake rather than a sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leijonmalm2017_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leijonmalm2017-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Baltic Sea's <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> is much lower than that of ocean water (which averages 3.5%), as a result of abundant freshwater runoff from the surrounding land (rivers, streams and alike), combined with the shallowness of the sea itself; runoff contributes roughly one-fortieth its total volume per year, as the volume of the basin is about 21,000 km<sup>3</sup> (5,000 cu mi) and yearly runoff is about 500 km<sup>3</sup> (120 cu mi).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The open surface waters of the Baltic Sea "proper" generally have a salinity of 0.3 to 0.9%, which is border-line <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a>. The flow of fresh water into the sea from approximately two hundred rivers and the introduction of salt from the southwest builds up a gradient of salinity in the Baltic Sea. The highest surface salinities, generally 0.7–0.9%, is in the southwestern-most part of the Baltic, in the Arkona and Bornholm Basins (the former located roughly between southeast <a href="/wiki/Zealand" title="Zealand">Zealand</a> and Bornholm, and the latter directly east of Bornholm). It gradually falls further east and north, reaching the lowest in the <a href="/wiki/Bothnian_Bay" title="Bothnian Bay">Bothnian Bay</a> at around 0.3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hydrogeography&amp;Oxygen_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hydrogeography&amp;Oxygen-37">[37]</a></sup> Drinking the surface water of the Baltic as a means of survival would actually hydrate the body instead of <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydrating</a>, as is the case with ocean water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[note 1]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>As salt water is denser than fresh water, the bottom of the Baltic Sea is saltier than the surface. This creates a vertical stratification of the water column, a <a href="/wiki/Halocline" title="Halocline">halocline</a>, that represents a barrier to the exchange of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and nutrients, and fosters completely separate maritime environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> The difference between the bottom and surface salinities vary depending on location. Overall it follows the same southwest to east and north pattern as the surface. At the bottom of the Arkona Basin (equalling depths greater than 40 m or 130 ft) and Bornholm Basin (depths greater than 80 m or 260 ft) it is typically 1.4–1.8%. Further east and north the salinity at the bottom is consistently lower, being the lowest in Bothnian Bay (depths greater than 120 m or 390 ft) where it is slightly below 0.4%, or only marginally higher than the surface in the same region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hydrogeography&amp;Oxygen_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hydrogeography&amp;Oxygen-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast, the salinity of the <a href="/wiki/Danish_straits" title="Danish straits">Danish straits</a>, which connect the Baltic Sea and Kattegat, tends to be significantly higher, but with major variations from year to year. For example, the surface and bottom salinity in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Belt" title="Great Belt">Great Belt</a> is typically around 2.0% and 2.8% respectively, which is only somewhat below that of the Kattegat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hydrogeography&amp;Oxygen_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hydrogeography&amp;Oxygen-37">[37]</a></sup> The water surplus caused by the continuous inflow of rivers and streams to the Baltic Sea means that there generally is a flow of brackish water out though the Danish straits to the Kattegat (and eventually the Atlantic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muus1999_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muus1999-40">[39]</a></sup> Significant flows in the opposite direction, salt water from the Kattegat through the Danish straits to the Baltic Sea, are less regular. From 1880 to 1980 inflows occurred on average six to seven times per decade. Since 1980 it has been much less frequent, although a very large inflow occurred in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OurBalticSea_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-OurBalticSea-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_tributaries">Major tributaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Major tributaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_of_the_Baltic_Sea" title="List of rivers of the Baltic Sea">List of rivers of the Baltic Sea</a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islands_and_archipelagoes">Islands and archipelagoes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Islands and archipelagoes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_in_the_Baltic_Sea" title="List of islands in the Baltic Sea">List of islands in the Baltic Sea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archipelago062009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="992" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Archipelago062009.jpg/300px-Archipelago062009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Archipelago062009.jpg/450px-Archipelago062009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Archipelago062009.jpg/600px-Archipelago062009.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archipelago062009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Skerry" title="Skerry">Skerries</a> form an integral and typical part of many of the <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelagos</a> of the Baltic Sea, such as these in the archipelago of the <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">Åland Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>.</div></div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_countries">Coastal countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Coastal countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Image-Lithuania_Juodkrante_sand_dunes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1706" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Image-Lithuania_Juodkrante_sand_dunes.jpg/220px-Image-Lithuania_Juodkrante_sand_dunes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Image-Lithuania_Juodkrante_sand_dunes.jpg/330px-Image-Lithuania_Juodkrante_sand_dunes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Image-Lithuania_Juodkrante_sand_dunes.jpg/440px-Image-Lithuania_Juodkrante_sand_dunes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Image-Lithuania_Juodkrante_sand_dunes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vast coastal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dunes" title="Dunes">dunes</a> are characteristic for large parts of the southern shore of the Baltic Sea. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kur%C5%A1i%C5%B3_Nerija_National_Park" title="Kuršių Nerija National Park">Kuršių Nerija National Park</a> in Lithuania <i>(pictured)</i> is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Curonian_Spit" title="Curonian Spit">Curonian Spit</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_density_in_the_Baltic_Sea_catchment_area.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="376" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Population_density_in_the_Baltic_Sea_catchment_area.svg/220px-Population_density_in_the_Baltic_Sea_catchment_area.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Population_density_in_the_Baltic_Sea_catchment_area.svg/330px-Population_density_in_the_Baltic_Sea_catchment_area.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Population_density_in_the_Baltic_Sea_catchment_area.svg/440px-Population_density_in_the_Baltic_Sea_catchment_area.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_density_in_the_Baltic_Sea_catchment_area.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population density in the Baltic Sea catchment area</div></div></div>

src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Flag_of_Ukraine.svg/23px-Flag_of_Ukraine.svg.png" </p><p>The Baltic sea drainage basin is roughly four times the surface area of the sea itself. About 48% of the region is forested, with Sweden and Finland containing the majority of the forest, especially around the Gulfs of Bothnia and Finland.
</p><p>About 20% of the land is used for agriculture and pasture, mainly in Poland and around the edge of the Baltic Proper, in Germany, Denmark and Sweden. About 17% of the basin is unused open land with another 8% of wetlands. Most of the latter are in the Gulfs of Bothnia and Finland.
</p><p>The rest of the land is heavily populated. About 85 million people live in the Baltic drainage basin, 15 million within 10 km (6 mi) of the coast and 29 million within 50 km (31 mi) of the coast. Around 22 million live in population centers of over 250,000. 90% of these are concentrated in the 10 km (6 mi) band around the coast. Of the nations containing all or part of the basin, Poland includes 45% of the 85 million, Russia 12%, Sweden 10% and the others less than 6% each.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A2%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD._-_panoramio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/%D0%A2%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD._-_panoramio.jpg/220px-%D0%A2%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD._-_panoramio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/%D0%A2%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD._-_panoramio.jpg/330px-%D0%A2%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD._-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/%D0%A2%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD._-_panoramio.jpg/440px-%D0%A2%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD._-_panoramio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A2%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%BD._-_panoramio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">Tallinn</a> in Estonia</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paasitorni_by_the_sea.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2420" data-file-width="3630" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Paasitorni_by_the_sea.jpg/220px-Paasitorni_by_the_sea.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Paasitorni_by_the_sea.jpg/330px-Paasitorni_by_the_sea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Paasitorni_by_the_sea.jpg/440px-Paasitorni_by_the_sea.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paasitorni_by_the_sea.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a> in Finland</div></div></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spb_06-2017_img01_Spit_of_Vasilievsky_Island.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2839" data-file-width="4258" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Spb_06-2017_img01_Spit_of_Vasilievsky_Island.jpg/220px-Spb_06-2017_img01_Spit_of_Vasilievsky_Island.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Spb_06-2017_img01_Spit_of_Vasilievsky_Island.jpg/330px-Spb_06-2017_img01_Spit_of_Vasilievsky_Island.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Spb_06-2017_img01_Spit_of_Vasilievsky_Island.jpg/440px-Spb_06-2017_img01_Spit_of_Vasilievsky_Island.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spb_06-2017_img01_Spit_of_Vasilievsky_Island.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vasilyevsky_Island" title="Vasilyevsky Island">Vasilyevsky Island</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, Russia</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Baltic_Sea" title="Geology of the Baltic Sea">Geology of the Baltic Sea</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:433px;max-width:433px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baltic_History_7500-BC.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1306" data-file-width="961" decoding="async" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Baltic_History_7500-BC.svg/250px-Baltic_History_7500-BC.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Baltic_History_7500-BC.svg/375px-Baltic_History_7500-BC.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Baltic_History_7500-BC.svg/500px-Baltic_History_7500-BC.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Ancylus_Lake" title="Ancylus Lake">Ancylus Lake</a> around 8700 years <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>. The relic of Scandinavian Glacier in white. The rivers <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Svea_%C3%A4lv&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Svea älv (page does not exist)">Svea älv</a> (Svea river) and <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ta_%C3%A4lv" title="Göta älv">Göta älv</a> formed an outlet to the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:177px;max-width:177px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suomi_jaakauden_jalkeen.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="849" decoding="async" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Suomi_jaakauden_jalkeen.png/175px-Suomi_jaakauden_jalkeen.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Suomi_jaakauden_jalkeen.png/263px-Suomi_jaakauden_jalkeen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Suomi_jaakauden_jalkeen.png/350px-Suomi_jaakauden_jalkeen.png 2x" width="175"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Much of modern <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> is former seabed or archipelago: illustrated are sea levels immediately after the last ice age.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Baltic_Sea" title="Geology of the Baltic Sea">Evolution of the Baltic Sea</a></th></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">
Pleistocene</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<a href="/wiki/Eemian_Sea" title="Eemian Sea">Eemian Sea</a> (130,000–115,000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>)<br/><a href="/wiki/Weichselian_glaciation" title="Weichselian glaciation">Ice sheets and seas</a> (115,000–12,600 BP)</td>
</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">
Holocene</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<a href="/wiki/Baltic_Ice_Lake" title="Baltic Ice Lake">Baltic Ice Lake</a> (12,600–10,300 BP)<br/><a href="/wiki/Yoldia_Sea" title="Yoldia Sea">Yoldia Sea</a> (10,300–9,500 BP)<br/><a href="/wiki/Ancylus_Lake" title="Ancylus Lake">Ancylus Lake</a> (9,500–8,000 BP)<br/><a href="/wiki/Mastogloia_Sea" title="Mastogloia Sea">Mastogloia Sea</a> (8,000–7,500 BP)<br/><a href="/wiki/Littorina_Sea" title="Littorina Sea">Littorina Sea</a> (7,500–4,000 BP)<br/><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Modern Baltic Sea</a> (4,000 BP–present)</td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Evolution_of_the_Baltic_Sea" title="Template:Evolution of the Baltic Sea"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Evolution_of_the_Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template talk:Evolution of the Baltic Sea (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Evolution_of_the_Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Baltic Sea somewhat resembles a <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">riverbed</a>, with two tributaries, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">Geological</a> surveys show that before the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, instead of the Baltic Sea, there was a wide plain around a great river that paleontologists call the <a href="/wiki/Eridanos_(geology)" title="Eridanos (geology)">Eridanos</a>. Several Pleistocene <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciation" title="Glaciation">glacial</a> episodes scooped out the river bed into the sea basin. By the time of the last, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eemian_Stage" title="Eemian Stage">Eemian Stage</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_isotopic_stage" title="Marine isotopic stage">MIS</a> 5e), the Eemian Sea was in place. Instead of a true sea, the Baltic can even today also be understood as the common <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuary</a> of all rivers flowing into it.
</p><p>From that time the waters underwent a geologic history summarized under the names listed below. Many of the stages are named after marine animals (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Littorina" title="Littorina">Littorina</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusk</a>) that are clear markers of changing water temperatures and salinity.
</p><p>The factors that determined the sea's characteristics were the submergence or emergence of the region due to the weight of ice and subsequent isostatic readjustment, and the connecting channels it found to the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>-<a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, either through the straits of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> or at what are now the large lakes of <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_Sea" title="Arctic Sea">Arctic Sea</a>.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Eemian_Sea" title="Eemian Sea">Eemian Sea</a>, 130,000–115,000 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_present" title="Before present">years ago</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baltic_Ice_Lake" title="Baltic Ice Lake">Baltic Ice Lake</a>, 12,600–10,300</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yoldia_Sea" title="Yoldia Sea">Yoldia Sea</a>, 10,300–9500</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ancylus_Lake" title="Ancylus Lake">Ancylus Lake</a>, 9,500–8,000</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mastogloia_Sea" title="Mastogloia Sea">Mastogloia Sea</a> 8,000–7,500</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Littorina_Sea" title="Littorina Sea">Littorina Sea</a>, 7,500–4,000</li>
<li>Post-Littorina Sea 4,000–present</li></ul>
<p>The land is still emerging <a href="/wiki/Isostasy" title="Isostasy">isostatically</a> from its depressed state, which was caused by the weight of ice during the last glaciation. The phenomenon is known as <a href="/wiki/Post-glacial_rebound" title="Post-glacial rebound">post-glacial rebound</a>. Consequently, the surface area and the depth of the sea are diminishing. The uplift is about eight millimetres per year on the Finnish coast of the northernmost Gulf of Bothnia. In the area, the former seabed is only gently sloping, leading to large areas of land being reclaimed in what are, geologically speaking, relatively short periods (decades and centuries).
</p>
<h3><span id="The_.22Baltic_Sea_anomaly.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='The_"Baltic_Sea_anomaly"'>The "Baltic Sea anomaly"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title='Edit section: The "Baltic Sea anomaly"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea_anomaly" title="Baltic Sea anomaly">Baltic Sea anomaly</a></div>
<p>The "Baltic Sea anomaly" refers to interpretations of an indistinct <a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a> image taken by Swedish salvage divers on the floor of the northern Baltic Sea in June 2011. The treasure hunters suggested the image showed an object with unusual features of seemingly extraordinary origin. Speculation published in <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_journalism" title="Tabloid journalism">tabloid newspapers</a> claimed that the object was a sunken <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UFO" title="UFO">UFO</a>. A consensus of experts and scientists say that the image most likely shows a natural <a href="/wiki/Geological_formation" title="Geological formation">geological formation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snopes_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Snopes-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowStuffWorks_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-HowStuffWorks-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LiveScience_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-LiveScience-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopMech_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopMech-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna_and_flora">Fauna and flora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Fauna and flora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_fish_in_Sweden" title="List of fish in Sweden">List of fish in Sweden</a></div>
<p>The fauna of the Baltic Sea is a mixture of marine and freshwater species. Among marine fishes are <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">Atlantic cod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_herring" title="Atlantic herring">Atlantic herring</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_hake" title="European hake">European hake</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_plaice" title="European plaice">European plaice</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_flounder" title="European flounder">European flounder</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shorthorn_sculpin" title="Shorthorn sculpin">shorthorn sculpin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turbot" title="Turbot">turbot</a>, and examples of freshwater species include <a href="/wiki/European_perch" title="European perch">European perch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_pike" title="Northern pike">northern pike</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coregonus" title="Coregonus">whitefish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_roach" title="Common roach">common roach</a>. Freshwater species may occur at outflows of rivers or streams in all coastal sections of the Baltic Sea. Otherwise marine species dominate in most sections of the Baltic, at least as far north as <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A4vle" title="Gävle">Gävle</a>, where less than one-tenth are freshwater species. Further north the pattern is inverted. In the Bothnian Bay, roughly two-thirds of the species are freshwater. In the far north of this bay, saltwater species are almost entirely absent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OurBalticSea_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-OurBalticSea-26">[26]</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/Common_starfish" title="Common starfish">common starfish</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shore_crab" title="Shore crab">shore crab</a>, two species that are very widespread along European coasts, are both unable to cope with the significantly lower salinity. Their range limit is west of Bornholm, meaning that they are absent from the vast majority of the Baltic Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OurBalticSea_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-OurBalticSea-26">[26]</a></sup> Some marine species, like the Atlantic cod and European flounder, can survive at relatively low salinities, but need higher salinities to breed, which therefore occurs in deeper parts of the Baltic Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nissling1997_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nissling1997-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Momigliano2018_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Momigliano2018-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a decrease in species richness from the Danish belts to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a>. The decreasing salinity along this path causes restrictions in both physiology and habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> At more than 600 species of invertebrates, fish, aquatic mammals, aquatic birds and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrophyte" title="Macrophyte">macrophytes</a>, the Arkona Basin (roughly between southeast Zealand and Bornholm) is far richer than other more eastern and northern basins in the Baltic Sea, which all have less than 400 species from these groups, with the exception of the Gulf of Finland with more than 750 species. However, even the most diverse sections of the Baltic Sea have far less species than the almost-full saltwater Kattegat, which is home to more than 1600 species from these groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OurBalticSea_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-OurBalticSea-26">[26]</a></sup> The lack of <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> has affected the marine species as compared with the Atlantic.
</p><p>Since the Baltic Sea is so young there are only two or three known <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> species: the brown alga <i><a href="/wiki/Fucus_radicans" title="Fucus radicans">Fucus radicans</a></i> and the flounder <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Platichthys_solemdali&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Platichthys solemdali (page does not exist)">Platichthys solemdali</a></i>. Both appear to have evolved in the Baltic basin and were only recognized as species in 2005 and 2018 respectively, having formerly been confused with more widespread relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Momigliano2018_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Momigliano2018-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pereyra2009_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pereyra2009-50">[49]</a></sup> The tiny <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Parvicardium_hauniense&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Parvicardium hauniense (page does not exist)">Copenhagen cockle</a> (<i>Parvicardium hauniense</i>), a rare mussel, is sometimes considered endemic, but has now been recorded in the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> However, some consider non-Baltic records to be misidentifications of juvenile <a href="/wiki/Lagoon_cockle" title="Lagoon cockle">lagoon cockles</a> (<i>Cerastoderma glaucum</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> Several widespread marine species have distinctive subpopulations in the Baltic Sea adapted to the low salinity, such as the Baltic Sea forms of the Atlantic herring and <a href="/wiki/Cyclopterus_lumpus" title="Cyclopterus lumpus">lumpsucker</a>, which are smaller than the widespread forms in the North Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muus1999_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Muus1999-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>A peculiar feature of the fauna is that it contains a number of glacial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relict_species" title="Relict species">relict species</a>, isolated populations of arctic species which have remained in the Baltic Sea since the last <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciation" title="Glaciation">glaciation</a>, such as the large isopod <i><a href="/wiki/Saduria_entomon" title="Saduria entomon">Saduria entomon</a></i>, the Baltic subspecies of <a href="/wiki/Ringed_seal" title="Ringed seal">ringed seal</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Fourhorn_sculpin" title="Fourhorn sculpin">fourhorn sculpin</a>. Some of these relicts are derived from <a href="/wiki/Glacial_lake" title="Glacial lake">glacial lakes</a>, such as  <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoporeia_affinis" title="Monoporeia affinis">Monoporeia affinis</a></i>, which is a main element in the <a href="/wiki/Benthos" title="Benthos">benthic fauna</a> of the low-salinity <a href="/wiki/Bothnian_Bay" title="Bothnian Bay">Bothnian Bay</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">Cetaceans</a> in Baltic Sea have been monitored by the <a href="/wiki/ASCOBANS" title="ASCOBANS">ASCOBANS</a>. Critically endangered populations of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_white-sided_dolphin" title="Atlantic white-sided dolphin">Atlantic white-sided dolphins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harbor_porpoise" title="Harbor porpoise">harbor porpoises</a> inhabit the sea where white-colored porpoises have been recorded,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> and occasionally oceanic and out-of-range species such as <a href="/wiki/Minke_whale" title="Minke whale">minke whales</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">bottlenose dolphins</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale" title="Beluga whale">beluga whales</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orcas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Beaked_whale" title="Beaked whale">beaked whales</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> visit the waters. In recent years, very small, but with increasing rates, <a href="/wiki/Fin_whale" title="Fin whale">fin whales</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Humpback_whale" title="Humpback whale">humpback whales</a> migrate into Baltic sea including mother and calf pair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Now extinct Atlantic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grey_whale" title="Grey whale">grey whales</a> (remains found from <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%A4s%C3%B6" title="Gräsö">Gräsö</a> along <a href="/wiki/Bothnian_Sea" title="Bothnian Sea">Bothnian Sea</a>/southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bothnian_Gulf" title="Bothnian Gulf">Bothnian Gulf</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ystad" title="Ystad">Ystad</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>) and eastern population of <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_right_whale" title="North Atlantic right whale">North Atlantic right whales</a> that is facing <a href="/wiki/Functional_extinction" title="Functional extinction">functional extinction</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> once migrated into Baltic Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other notable <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Basking_shark" title="Basking shark">basking sharks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_status">Environmental status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Environmental status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea_hypoxia" title="Baltic Sea hypoxia">Baltic Sea hypoxia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phytoplankton_bloom_in_the_Baltic_Sea_(July_3,_2001).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="803" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Phytoplankton_bloom_in_the_Baltic_Sea_%28July_3%2C_2001%29.jpg/220px-Phytoplankton_bloom_in_the_Baltic_Sea_%28July_3%2C_2001%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Phytoplankton_bloom_in_the_Baltic_Sea_%28July_3%2C_2001%29.jpg/330px-Phytoplankton_bloom_in_the_Baltic_Sea_%28July_3%2C_2001%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Phytoplankton_bloom_in_the_Baltic_Sea_%28July_3%2C_2001%29.jpg/440px-Phytoplankton_bloom_in_the_Baltic_Sea_%28July_3%2C_2001%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phytoplankton_bloom_in_the_Baltic_Sea_(July_3,_2001).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">Phytoplankton</a> <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algal bloom</a> in the Baltic Proper, July 2001</div></div></div>
<p>Satellite images taken in July 2010 revealed a massive <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algal bloom</a> covering 377,000 square kilometres (146,000 sq mi) in the Baltic Sea. The area of the bloom extended from Germany and Poland to Finland. Researchers of the phenomenon have indicated that algal blooms have occurred every summer for decades. Fertilizer runoff from surrounding agricultural land has exacerbated the problem and led to increased <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Approximately 100,000 km<sup>2</sup> (38,610 sq mi) of the Baltic's seafloor (a quarter of its total area) is a variable <a href="/wiki/Dead_zone_(ecology)" title="Dead zone (ecology)">dead zone</a>. The more saline (and therefore denser) water remains on the bottom, isolating it from surface waters and the atmosphere. This leads to decreased oxygen concentrations within the zone. It is mainly bacteria that grow in it, digesting organic material and releasing hydrogen sulfide. Because of this large anaerobic zone, the seafloor ecology differs from that of the neighbouring Atlantic.
</p><p>Plans to artificially oxygenate areas of the Baltic that have experienced eutrophication have been proposed by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Gothenburg" title="University of Gothenburg">University of Gothenburg</a> and Inocean AB. The proposal intends to use wind-driven pumps to inject oxygen (air) into waters at, or around, 130m below sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea_cruiseferries" title="Baltic Sea cruiseferries">Baltic Sea cruiseferries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ports_of_the_Baltic_Sea" title="Ports of the Baltic Sea">Ports of the Baltic Sea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palangos_tiltas1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="979" data-file-width="1306" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Palangos_tiltas1.jpg/220px-Palangos_tiltas1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Palangos_tiltas1.jpg/330px-Palangos_tiltas1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Palangos_tiltas1.jpg/440px-Palangos_tiltas1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palangos_tiltas1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pedestrian pier at <a href="/wiki/Palanga" title="Palanga">Palanga</a>, the most popular sea resort in <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a></div></div></div>
<p>Construction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Belt_Bridge" title="Great Belt Bridge">Great Belt Bridge</a> in Denmark (completed 1997) and the <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund_Bridge" title="Øresund Bridge">Øresund Bridge</a>-Tunnel (completed 1999), linking Denmark with Sweden, provided a highway and railroad connection between Sweden and the Danish mainland (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jutland_Peninsula" title="Jutland Peninsula">Jutland Peninsula</a>, precisely the <a href="/wiki/Zealand" title="Zealand">Zealand</a>). The undersea tunnel of the Øresund Bridge-Tunnel provides for navigation of large ships into and out of the Baltic Sea. The Baltic Sea is the main trade route for export of Russian petroleum. Many of the countries neighboring the Baltic Sea have been concerned about this, since a major oil leak in a seagoing tanker would be disastrous for the Baltic—given the slow exchange of water. The tourism industry surrounding the Baltic Sea is naturally concerned about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_pollution" title="Oil pollution">oil pollution</a>.
</p><p>Much shipbuilding is carried out in the shipyards around the Baltic Sea. The largest shipyards are at <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gdynia" title="Gdynia">Gdynia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Szczecin" title="Szczecin">Szczecin</a>, Poland; <a href="/wiki/Kiel" title="Kiel">Kiel</a>, Germany; <a href="/wiki/Karlskrona" title="Karlskrona">Karlskrona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malm%C3%B6" title="Malmö">Malmö</a>, Sweden; <a href="/wiki/Rauma,_Finland" title="Rauma, Finland">Rauma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">Turku</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>, Finland; <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ventspils" title="Ventspils">Ventspils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Liep%C4%81ja" title="Liepāja">Liepāja</a>, Latvia; <a href="/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da" title="Klaipėda">Klaipėda</a>, Lithuania; and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, Russia.
</p><p>There are several cargo and passenger ferries that operate on the Baltic Sea, such as <a href="/wiki/Scandlines" title="Scandlines">Scandlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silja_Line" title="Silja Line">Silja Line</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polferries" title="Polferries">Polferries</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Line" title="Viking Line">Viking Line</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tallink" title="Tallink">Tallink</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Superfast_Ferries" title="Superfast Ferries">Superfast Ferries</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B6_%D0%B2_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B5_-_panoramio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/%D0%9F%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B6_%D0%B2_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B5_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-%D0%9F%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B6_%D0%B2_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B5_-_panoramio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/%D0%9F%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B6_%D0%B2_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B5_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-%D0%9F%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B6_%D0%B2_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B5_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/%D0%9F%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B6_%D0%B2_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B5_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-%D0%9F%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B6_%D0%B2_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B5_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B6_%D0%B2_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B5_-_panoramio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Svetlogorsk,_Kaliningrad_Oblast" title="Svetlogorsk, Kaliningrad Oblast">Svetlogorsk</a> resort town in <a href="/wiki/Kaliningrad_Oblast" title="Kaliningrad Oblast">Kaliningrad Oblast</a>, Russia</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mrzezyno_east_beach_2010-07_A.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2585" data-file-width="3874" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mrzezyno_east_beach_2010-07_A.jpg/220px-Mrzezyno_east_beach_2010-07_A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mrzezyno_east_beach_2010-07_A.jpg/330px-Mrzezyno_east_beach_2010-07_A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Mrzezyno_east_beach_2010-07_A.jpg/440px-Mrzezyno_east_beach_2010-07_A.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mrzezyno_east_beach_2010-07_A.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mrze%C5%BCyno" title="Mrzeżyno">Mrzeżyno</a> beach in Poland</div></div></div>

<li><a href="/wiki/Ueckerm%C3%BCnde" title="Ueckermünde">Ueckermünde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Travem%C3%BCnde" title="Travemünde">Travemünde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/J%C5%ABrmala" title="Jūrmala">Jūrmala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nida,_Lithuania" title="Nida, Lithuania">Nida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Palanga" title="Palanga">Palanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a></li>

<p>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Helsinki_Convention">The Helsinki Convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: The Helsinki Convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1974_Convention">1974 Convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: 1974 Convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For the first time ever, all the sources of pollution around an entire sea were made subject to a single convention, signed in 1974 by the then seven Baltic coastal states. The 1974 Convention entered into force on 3 May 1980.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1992_Convention">1992 Convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltic_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: 1992 Convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Convention_on_the_Protection_of_the_Marine_Environment_of_the_Baltic_Sea_Area" title="Helsinki Convention on the Protection of the Marine Environment of the Baltic Sea Area">Helsinki Convention on the Protection of the Marine Environment of the Baltic Sea Area</a></div>
<p>In the light of political changes and developments in international environmental and maritime law, a new convention was signed in 1992 by all the states bordering on the Baltic Sea, and the European Community. After ratification the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Protection_of_the_Marine_Environment_of_the_Baltic_Sea_Area" title="Convention on the Protection of the Marine Environment of the Baltic Sea Area">Convention</a> entered into force on 17 January 2000. The Convention covers the whole of the Baltic Sea area, including inland waters and the water of the sea itself, as well as the seabed. Measures are also taken in the whole catchment area of the Baltic Sea to reduce land-based pollution. The Convention on the Protection of the Marine Environment of the Baltic Sea Area, 1992, entered into force on 17 January 2000.
</p><p>The governing body of the Convention is the <a href="/wiki/HELCOM" title="HELCOM">Helsinki Commission</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> also known as HELCOM, or Baltic Marine Environment Protection Commission. The present contracting parties are Denmark, Estonia, the European Community, Finland, Germany, Latvia, Lithuania, Poland, Russia and Sweden.
</p><p>The ratification instruments were deposited by the European Community, Germany, Latvia and Sweden in 1994, by Estonia and Finland in 1995, by Denmark in 1996, by Lithuania in 1997 and by Poland and Russia in November 1999.
</p>

<li>Lisk, Jill. <i>The Struggle for Supremacy in the Baltic, 1600–1725</i> (U of London Press, 1967).</li>
<li>Roberts, Michael. <i>The Early Vasas: A History of Sweden, 1523–1611</i> (Cambridge UP, 1968).</li>
<li>Rystad, Göran, Klaus-R. Böhme, and Wilhelm M. Carlgren, eds. <i>In Quest of Trade and Security: The Baltic in Power Politics, 1500–1990.</i> Vol. 1, 1500–1890. Stockholm: Probus, 1994.</li>
<li>Salmon, Patrick, and Tony Barrow, eds. <i>Britain and the Baltic: Studies in Commercial, Political and Cultural Relations</i> (Sunderland University Press, 2003).</li>
<li>Stiles, Andrina. <i>Sweden and the Baltic 1523–1721</i> (1992).</li>
<li>Thomson, Erik. “Beyond the Military State: Sweden's Great Power Period in Recent Historiography.” <i>History Compass</i> 9 (2011): 269–283. DOI: 10.1111/j.1478-0542.2011.00761.x</li>
<li>Tielhof, Milja van. The “Mother of All Trades”: The Baltic Grain Trade in Amsterdam from the Late 16th to Early 19th Century. Leiden, The Netherlands: Brill, 2002.</li>
